noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/\xcb\x88/: primary stress follows">\xcb\x88</span><span title="&#39;l&#39; in &#39;lie&#39;">l</span><span title="/\xc9\x9b/: &#39;e&#39; in &#39;dress&#39;">\xc9\x9b</span><span title="&#39;s&#39; in &#39;sigh&#39;">s</span><span title="&#39;t&#39; in &#39;tie&#39;">t</span><span title="/\xc9\x99r/: &#39;er&#39; in &#39;letter&#39;">\xc9\x99r</span></span>/</a></span><small class="nowrap">&#160;(<span class="unicode haudio"><span class="fn"><span style="white-space:nowrap"><a href="/wiki/File:Leicester.ogg" title="About this sound"><img alt="About this sound" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" width="11" height="11" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="20" data-file-height="20" /></a>&#160;</span><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/98/Leicester.ogg" class="internal" title="Leicester.ogg">listen</a></span></span>)</small></span> "Lester")<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> is a <a href="/wiki/City_status_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="City status in the United Kingdom">city</a> and <a href="/wiki/Unitary_authorities_of_England" title="Unitary authorities of England">unitary authority area</a> in the <a href="/wiki/East_Midlands" title="East Midlands">East Midlands</a> of <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">England</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/County_town" title="County town">county town</a> of <a href="/wiki/Leicestershire" title="Leicestershire">Leicestershire</a>. The city lies on the <a href="/wiki/River_Soar" title="River Soar">River Soar</a> and close to the eastern end of the <a href="/wiki/National_Forest,_England" class="mw-redirect" title="National Forest, England">National Forest</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The 2016 mid year estimate of the population of the City of Leicester <a href="/wiki/Unitary_authority" title="Unitary authority">unitary authority</a> was 348,300, an increase of approximately 18,500 (<img alt="Increase" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Increase2.svg/11px-Increase2.svg.png" title="Increase" width="11" height="11" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Increase2.svg/17px-Increase2.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Increase2.svg/22px-Increase2.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="300" data-file-height="300" /> 5.6%) from the 2011 census figure of 329,839, making it the most populous municipality in the East Midlands region. The associated <a href="/wiki/Urban_area#United_Kingdom" title="Urban area">urban area</a> is also the 11th most populous in England and the <a href="/wiki/List_of_urban_areas_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="List of urban areas in the United Kingdom">13th most populous in the United Kingdom</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Leicester is at the intersection of two major railway lines\xe2\x80\x94the north/south <a href="/wiki/Midland_Main_Line" title="Midland Main Line">Midland Main Line</a> and the east/west <a href="/wiki/Birmingham" title="Birmingham">Birmingham</a> to <a href="/wiki/London_Stansted_Airport" title="London Stansted Airport">London Stansted</a> <a href="/wiki/CrossCountry" title="CrossCountry">CrossCountry</a> line; as well as the confluence of the <a href="/wiki/M1_motorway" title="M1 motorway">M1</a>/<a href="/wiki/M69_motorway" title="M69 motorway">M69 motorways</a> and the <a href="/wiki/A6_road_(England)" title="A6 road (England)">A6</a>/<a href="/wiki/A46_road_(England)" class="mw-redirect" title="A46 road (England)">A46</a> trunk routes. Leicester is the home to <a href="/wiki/Association_football" title="Association football">football</a> club <a href="/wiki/Leicester_City_F.C." title="Leicester City F.C.">Leicester City</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rugby_Union" class="mw-redirect" title="Rugby Union">rugby</a> club <a href="/wiki/Leicester_Tigers" title="Leicester Tigers">Leicester Tigers</a>.\n</p>\n<div id="toc" class="toc"><input class="toclevel-1 tocsection-47"><a href="#Notable_people"><span class="tocnumber">14</span> <span class="toctext">Notable people</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-48"><a <span class="toctext">Twin cities</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-50"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">17</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-51"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">18</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Name">Name</span></h2>\n<p>The name of Leicester is recorded in the 9th-century <i><a href="/wiki/Historia_Brittonum" title="Historia Brittonum">History of the Britons</a></i> as <i><span class="nowrap">Cair Lerion</span></i> (whence <a href="/wiki/Welsh_placenames" class="mw-redirect" title="Welsh placenames">Welsh</a> <i><i lang="cy" title="Welsh language text">Caerl\xc5\xb7r</i></i>), and in the <i><a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxon_Chronicle" title="Anglo-Saxon Chronicle">Anglo-Saxon Chronicle</a></i> as <i><i lang="ang" title="Old English language text">Ligora-ceastre</i></i>. In the <i><a href="/wiki/Domesday_Book" title="Domesday Book">Domesday Book</a></i> of 1086, it is recorded as <i><i lang="ang" title="Old English language text">Ledecestre</i></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-tommyB_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tommyB-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> The first element of the name, <i>Ligora</i> or <i>Legora</i>, is explained as a <a href="/wiki/Brittonic_languages" title="Brittonic languages">Brittonic</a> river name,  in a suggestion going back to <a href="/wiki/William_Somner" title="William Somner">William Somner</a> (1701) an earlier name of the <a href="/wiki/River_Soar" title="River Soar">River Soar</a>, cognate with the name of the <i><a href="/wiki/Loire" title="Loire">Loire</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Stevenson1918_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stevenson1918-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> The second element of the name comes from the Latin <i><a href="/wiki/Castrum" class="mw-redirect" title="Castrum">castrum</a></i> which is reflected in both Welsh <i>cair</i> and Anglo-Saxon <i>ceastre</i>.\n</p><p>Based on the Welsh name (given as <i>Kaerleir</i>), <a href="/wiki/Geoffrey_of_Monmouth" title="Geoffrey of Monmouth">Geoffrey of Monmouth</a> proposes a king <a href="/wiki/Leir_of_Britain" title="Leir of Britain">Leir of Britain</a> as an <a href="/wiki/Eponymous_founder" class="mw-redirect" title="Eponymous founder">eponymous founder</a> in his <i><a href="/wiki/Historia_Regum_Britanniae" title="Historia Regum Britanniae">Historia Regum Britanniae</a></i> (12th century).<sup id="cite_ref-Geoffrey211_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Geoffrey211-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_Leicester" title="Timeline of Leicester">Timeline of Leicester</a> and <a href="/wiki/History_of_Leicestershire" title="History of Leicestershire">History of Leicestershire</a></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Prehistory">Prehistory</span></h3>\n<p>Leicester is one of the oldest cities in England, with a history going back at least two millennia.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> The <a href="/wiki/Britons_(Celtic_people)" class="mw-redirect" title="Britons (Celtic people)">native</a> <a href="/wiki/British_Iron_Age" title="British Iron Age">Iron Age</a> settlement encountered by the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Romans" class="mw-redirect" title="Ancient Romans">Romans</a> at the site seems to have developed in the 2nd or 1st centuries <a href="/wiki/Anno_Domini" title="Anno Domini">BC</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template"><span style="white-space: nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Link_rot" title="Wikipedia:Link rot"><span title="&#160;Dead link since July 2017">dead link</span></a></i>&#93;</span></sup> Little is known about this settlement or the condition of the <a href="/wiki/River_Soar" title="River Soar">River Soar</a> at this time, although <a href="/wiki/Roundhouse_(dwelling)" title="Roundhouse (dwelling)">roundhouses</a> from this era have been excavated and seem to have clustered along roughly 8 hectares (20 acres) of the east bank of the Soar above its confluence with the <a href="/wiki/River_Trent" title="River Trent">Trent</a>. This area of the Soar was split into two channels: a main stream to the east and a narrower channel on the west, with a presumably marshy island between. The settlement seems to have controlled a ford across the larger channel. The <a href="/wiki/Ratae_Corieltauvorum#Name" title="Ratae Corieltauvorum">later Roman name</a> was a <a href="/wiki/Latinization_of_names" class="mw-redirect" title="Latinization of names">latinate</a> form of the <a href="/wiki/Common_Brittonic" title="Common Brittonic">Brittonic</a> word for "ramparts" (cf. <a href="/wiki/Irish_Gaelic" class="mw-redirect" title="Irish Gaelic">Gaelic</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Ringfort" title="Ringfort">rath</a></i> and the nearby villages of <a href="/wiki/Ratby" title="Ratby">Ratby</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ratcliffe-upon-Soar" class="mw-redirect" title="Ratcliffe-upon-Soar">Ratcliffe</a><sup id="cite_ref-tommyA_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tommyA-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>), suggesting the site was an <a href="/wiki/Oppidum" title="Oppidum">oppidum</a>. The plural form of the name suggests it was initially composed of several villages.<sup id="cite_ref-tommyA_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tommyA-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/List_of_Celtic_tribes" class="mw-redirect" title="List of Celtic tribes">Celtic tribe</a> holding the area was later recorded as the "<a href="/wiki/Coritani" class="mw-redirect" title="Coritani">Coritanians</a>" but an inscription recovered in 1983 showed this to have been a corruption of the original "<a href="/wiki/Corieltauvi" title="Corieltauvi">Corieltauvians</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-TLAHS_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TLAHS-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-AJ_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AJ-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> The Corieltauvians are believed to have ruled over roughly the area of the <a href="/wiki/East_Midlands" title="East Midlands">East Midlands</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Roman">Roman</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Jewry_Wall_ruins_Apr10.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Jewry_Wall_ruins_Apr10.jpg/220px-Jewry_Wall_ruins_Apr10.jpg" width="220" height="158" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Jewry_Wall_ruins_Apr10.jpg/330px-Jewry_Wall_ruins_Apr10.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Jewry_Wall_ruins_Apr10.jpg/440px-Jewry_Wall_ruins_Apr10.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5030" data-file-height="3603" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Jewry_Wall_ruins_Apr10.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/St_Nicholas%27_Church,_Leicester" title="St Nicholas&#39; Church, Leicester">St&#160;Nicholas\'s Church</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Jewry_Wall" title="Jewry Wall">Jewry Wall</a>.</div></div></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ratae_Corieltauvorum" title="Ratae Corieltauvorum">Ratae Corieltauvorum</a></div>\n<p>It is believed that the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">Romans</a> arrived in the Leicester area around <a href="/wiki/Anno_Domini" title="Anno Domini">AD</a> 47, during their <a href="/wiki/Roman_conquest_of_Britain" title="Roman conquest of Britain">conquest</a> of <a href="/wiki/Roman_Britain" title="Roman Britain">southern Britain</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Richard3part2_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Richard3part2-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> The Corieltauvian settlement lay near a bridge on the <a href="/wiki/Fosse_Way" title="Fosse Way">Fosse Way</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Roman_road" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman road">Roman road</a> between the legionary camps at <a href="/wiki/Isca_Dumnoniorum" title="Isca Dumnoniorum">Isca</a> (<a href="/wiki/Exeter" title="Exeter">Exeter</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Lindum_Colonia" title="Lindum Colonia">Lindum</a> (<a href="/wiki/Lincoln,_England" title="Lincoln, England">Lincoln</a>). It remains unclear whether the Romans fortified and garrisoned the location, but it slowly developed from around the year 50 onwards as the <a href="/wiki/Civitas" title="Civitas">tribal capital</a> of the Corieltauvians under the name <a href="/wiki/Ratae_Corieltauvorum" title="Ratae Corieltauvorum">Ratae Corieltauvorum</a>. In the 2nd century, it received a <a href="/wiki/Forum_(Roman)" title="Forum (Roman)">forum</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thermae" title="Thermae">bathhouse</a>. In 2013, the discovery of a Roman cemetery found just outside the old city walls and dating back to <span class="smallcaps"><span style="font-variant: small-caps; text-transform: lowercase;">AD</span></span>&#160;300 was announced.<sup id="cite_ref-Richard3part2_17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Richard3part2-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> The remains of the <a href="/wiki/Thermae" title="Thermae">baths</a> of Roman Leicester can be seen at the <a href="/wiki/Jewry_Wall" title="Jewry Wall">Jewry Wall</a>; recovered artifacts are displayed at the <a href="/wiki/Jewry_Wall_Museum" title="Jewry Wall Museum">adjacent museum</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Medieval">Medieval</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Leicester_Cathedral_south_facade.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Leicester_Cathedral_south_facade.jpg/220px-Leicester_Cathedral_south_facade.jpg" width="220" height="220" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Leicester_Cathedral_south_facade.jpg/330px-Leicester_Cathedral_south_facade.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Leicester_Cathedral_south_facade.jpg/440px-Leicester_Cathedral_south_facade.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2643" data-file-height="2643" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Leicester_Cathedral_south_facade.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Leicester_Cathedral" title="Leicester Cathedral">Leicester Cathedral</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Knowledge of the town following the <a href="/wiki/Roman_withdrawal_from_Britain" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman withdrawal from Britain">Roman withdrawal from Britain</a> is limited. Certainly there is some continuation of occupation of the town, though on a much reduced scale in the 5th and 6th centuries. Its memory was preserved as the <i><span class="nowrap">Cair Lerion</span></i><sup id="cite_ref-mommy_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mommy-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> of the <i><a href="/wiki/Historia_Brittonum" title="Historia Brittonum">History of the Britons</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-nashford_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nashford-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> Following the <a href="/wiki/Saxon_invasion_of_Britain" class="mw-redirect" title="Saxon invasion of Britain">Saxon invasion of Britain</a>, Leicester was occupied by the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Angles" title="Middle Angles">Middle Angles</a> and subsequently administered by the kingdom of <a href="/wiki/Mercia" title="Mercia">Mercia</a>. It was elevated to a <a href="/wiki/Bishop_of_Leicester" title="Bishop of Leicester">bishopric</a> in either 679 or 680; this see survived until the 9th century, when Leicester was captured by <a href="/wiki/Danes_(Germanic_people)" class="mw-redirect" title="Danes (Germanic people)">Danish</a> <a href="/wiki/Viking_invasions_of_Britain" class="mw-redirect" title="Viking invasions of Britain">Vikings</a>. Their settlement became one of the <a href="/wiki/Five_Burghs" class="mw-redirect" title="Five Burghs">Five Burghs</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Danelaw" title="Danelaw">Danelaw</a>, although this position was short-lived. The Saxon bishop, meanwhile, fled to <a href="/wiki/Dorchester-on-Thames" class="mw-redirect" title="Dorchester-on-Thames">Dorchester-on-Thames</a> and Leicester did not become a bishopric again until the Church of <span class="nowrap">St Martin</span> became <a href="/wiki/Leicester_Cathedral" title="Leicester Cathedral">Leicester Cathedral</a> in 1927. The settlement was recorded under the name <i>Ligeraceaster</i> in the early 10th century.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Leicester_Guildhall.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Leicester_Guildhall.jpg/220px-Leicester_Guildhall.jpg" width="220" height="153" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Leicester_Guildhall.jpg/330px-Leicester_Guildhall.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Leicester_Guildhall.jpg/440px-Leicester_Guildhall.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4850" data-file-height="3372" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Leicester_Guildhall.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Leicester_Guildhall" title="Leicester Guildhall">Leicester Guildhall</a>, dating from the 14th century</div></div></div>\n<p>Following the <a href="/wiki/Norman_Conquest" class="mw-redirect" title="Norman Conquest">Norman conquest</a>, Leicester was recorded by <a href="/wiki/William_I_of_England" class="mw-redirect" title="William I of England">William</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/Domesday_Book" title="Domesday Book">Domesday Book</a> as <i>Ledecestre</i>. It was noted as a city (<i>civitas</i>) but lost this status in the 11th century owing to power struggles between the <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church_in_England" class="mw-redirect" title="Catholic Church in England">Church</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Aristocracy" title="Aristocracy">aristocracy</a><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> and did not become a legal city again until 1919.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Geoffrey_of_Monmouth" title="Geoffrey of Monmouth">Geoffrey of Monmouth</a> composed his <i><a href="/wiki/Historia_Regum_Britanniae" title="Historia Regum Britanniae">History of the Kings of Britain</a></i> around the year 1136, naming a <a href="/wiki/Leir_of_Britain" title="Leir of Britain">King Leir</a> as an <a href="/wiki/Eponymous_founder" class="mw-redirect" title="Eponymous founder">eponymous founder</a> figure.<sup id="cite_ref-laleir_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-laleir-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> According to Geoffrey\'s narrative, <a href="/wiki/Queen_Cordelia" class="mw-redirect" title="Queen Cordelia">Cordelia</a> had buried her father beneath the river in a chamber dedicated to <a href="/wiki/Janus" title="Janus">Janus</a> and his feast day was an annual celebration.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>When <a href="/wiki/Simon_de_Montfort,_6th_Earl_of_Leicester" title="Simon de Montfort, 6th Earl of Leicester">Simon de Montfort</a> became Lord of Leicester in 1231, he gave the city a grant to expel the Jewish population<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> "in my time or in the time of any of my heirs to the end of the world". He justified his action as being "for the good of my soul, and for the souls of my ancestors and successors".<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> Leicester\'s Jews were allowed to move to the eastern suburbs, which were controlled by de Montfort\'s great-aunt and rival, Margaret, Countess of Winchester, after she took advice from the scholar and cleric <a href="/wiki/Robert_Grosseteste" title="Robert Grosseteste">Robert Grosseteste</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> There is evidence that Jews remained there until 1253, and perhaps enforcement of the banishment within the city was not rigorously enforced. De Montfort however issued a second edict for the expulsion of Leicester\'s Jews in 1253, after Grosseteste\'s death.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> De Montfort\'s many acts of anti-Jewish persecution in Leicester and elsewhere mass murder were part of a <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Jews_in_England_(1066%E2%80%931290)#Increasing_persecution,_13th_century" title="History of the Jews in England (1066\xe2\x80\x931290)">wider pattern</a> that led to the <a href="/wiki/Edict_of_Expulsion" title="Edict of Expulsion">expulsion of the Jewry</a> from England in 1290.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>During the C14th the earls of Leicester and Lancaster enhanced the prestige of the town. <a href="/wiki/Henry,_3rd_Earl_of_Lancaster" title="Henry, 3rd Earl of Lancaster">Henry, 3rd Earl of Lancaster</a> and of Leicester founded a hospital for the poor and infirm in the area to the south of the castle now known as The Newarke (the "new work"). Henry\'s son, the great <a href="/wiki/Henry_of_Grosmont" class="mw-redirect" title="Henry of Grosmont">Henry of Grosmont</a>, 4th Earl of Lancaster and of Leicester, who was made first Duke of Lancaster,  enlarged and enhanced his father\'s foundation, and built the collegiate Church of the Annunciation of Our Lady of The Newarke.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> This church (a little of which survives in the basement of the Hawthorn Building of De Montfort University) was destroyed during the reign of King Edward VI. It became an important pilgrimage site because it housed a thorn said to be from the Crown of Thorns, given to the Duke by the King of France. The church (described by Leland in the C16th as "not large but exceeding fair") also became, effectively, a Lancastrian mausoleum.\nDuke Henry\'s daughter <a href="/wiki/Blanche_of_Lancaster" title="Blanche of Lancaster">Blanche of Lancaster</a> married <a href="/wiki/John_of_Gaunt" title="John of Gaunt">John of Gaunt</a> and their son Henry Bolingbroke became <a href="/wiki/Henry_IV_of_England" title="Henry IV of England">King Henry IV</a> when he deposed King Richard II. The Church of the Annunciation was the burial place of Duke Henry, who had earlier had his father re-interred here. Later it became the burial place of <a href="/wiki/Constance_of_Castile,_Duchess_of_Lancaster" title="Constance of Castile, Duchess of Lancaster">Constance of Castile, Duchess of Lancaster</a> (second wife of John of Gaunt) and of <a href="/wiki/Mary_de_Bohun" title="Mary de Bohun">Mary de Bohun</a>, first wife of Henry Bolingbroke (Henry IV) and mother of King Henry V (she did not become queen because she died before Bolingbroke became king). John of Gaunt died at Leicester Castle in 1399. When his son became king, the Earldom of Leicester and the Duchy of Lancaster became royal titles (and the latter remains so).\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Magazine_Gateway,_Leicester.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/The_Magazine_Gateway%2C_Leicester.jpg/220px-The_Magazine_Gateway%2C_Leicester.jpg" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/The_Magazine_Gateway%2C_Leicester.jpg/330px-The_Magazine_Gateway%2C_Leicester.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/The_Magazine_Gateway%2C_Leicester.jpg/440px-The_Magazine_Gateway%2C_Leicester.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="680" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Magazine_Gateway,_Leicester.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Newarke Gateway or Magazine Gateway.</div></div></div>\n<p>At the end of the <a href="/wiki/War_of_the_Roses" class="mw-redirect" title="War of the Roses">War of the Roses</a>, <a href="/wiki/List_of_English_kings" class="mw-redirect" title="List of English kings">King</a> <a href="/wiki/Richard_III_of_England" title="Richard III of England">Richard III</a> was buried in Leicester\'s <a href="/wiki/Greyfriars,_Leicester" title="Greyfriars, Leicester">Greyfriars Church</a> a Franciscan Friary and Church which was demolished after its dissolution in 1538. The site of that church is now covered by more modern buildings and a car park. There was a legend his corpse had been cast into the <a href="/wiki/River_Soar" title="River Soar">river</a>, while some historians<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> argued his tomb and remains were destroyed during the <a href="/wiki/Dissolution_of_the_Monasteries" title="Dissolution of the Monasteries">dissolution of the monasteries</a> under <a href="/wiki/Henry_VIII_of_England" title="Henry VIII of England">Henry VIII</a>. However, in September 2012, an archaeological investigation of the car park revealed a skeleton<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> which <a href="/wiki/DNA_testing" class="mw-redirect" title="DNA testing">DNA testing</a> helped verify to be related to two descendants of Richard III\'s sister.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>  It was concluded that the skeleton was that of Richard III because of the DNA evidence and the shape of the spine. In 2015 Richard III was reburied in pride of place near the high altar in <a href="/wiki/Leicester_Cathedral" title="Leicester Cathedral">Leicester Cathedral</a>.\n</p>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Exhumation_and_reburial_of_Richard_III_of_England" title="Exhumation and reburial of Richard III of England">Exhumation and reburial of Richard III of England</a></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern">Modern</span></h3>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Tudor">Tudor</span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Leicester_Abbey_nave_and_cloister.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Leicester_Abbey_nave_and_cloister.jpg/220px-Leicester_Abbey_nave_and_cloister.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Leicester_Abbey_nave_and_cloister.jpg/330px-Leicester_Abbey_nave_and_cloister.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Leicester_Abbey_nave_and_cloister.jpg/440px-Leicester_Abbey_nave_and_cloister.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3000" data-file-height="2008" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Leicester_Abbey_nave_and_cloister.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Leicester_Abbey" title="Leicester Abbey">Leicester Abbey</a> ruins, now part of <a href="/wiki/Abbey_Park,_Leicester" title="Abbey Park, Leicester">Abbey Park</a></div></div></div>\n<p>On 4 November 1530, <a href="/wiki/Cardinal_Thomas_Wolsey" class="mw-redirect" title="Cardinal Thomas Wolsey">Cardinal Thomas Wolsey</a> was arrested on charges of treason and taken from <a href="/wiki/York_Place" class="mw-redirect" title="York Place">York Place</a>. On his way south to face dubious justice at the <a href="/wiki/Tower_of_London" title="Tower of London">Tower of London</a>, he fell ill. The group escorting him was concerned enough to stop at Leicester to rest at Leicester Abbey. There, Wolsey\'s condition quickly worsened. He died on 29 November 1530 and was buried at <a href="/wiki/Leicester_Abbey" title="Leicester Abbey">Leicester Abbey</a>, now <a href="/wiki/Abbey_Park,_Leicestershire" class="mw-redirect" title="Abbey Park, Leicestershire">Abbey Park</a>.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Lady_Jane_Grey" title="Lady Jane Grey">Lady Jane Grey</a>, who claimed the English throne for nine days in June 1553, was born at <a href="/wiki/Bradgate_Park" title="Bradgate Park">Bradgate Park</a> near Leicester around 1536.<sup id="cite_ref-royweb_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-royweb-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Queen <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_I" class="mw-redirect" title="Elizabeth I">Elizabeth I</a>\'s intimate and former suitor, <a href="/wiki/Robert_Dudley,_1st_Earl_of_Leicester" title="Robert Dudley, 1st Earl of Leicester">Robert Dudley</a>, was given the <a href="/wiki/Earldom_of_Leicester" class="mw-redirect" title="Earldom of Leicester">Earldom of Leicester</a>.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Stuart">Stuart</span></h4>\n<p>The Corporation of Leicester opposed the efforts of <a href="/wiki/Charles_I_of_England" title="Charles I of England">Charles I of England</a> to disafforest the nearby <a href="/wiki/Leicester_Forest" title="Leicester Forest">Leicester Forest</a>, believing them to be likely to throw many of its residents into poverty and need of relief. <a href="/wiki/Miles_Fleetwood" title="Miles Fleetwood">Sir Miles Fleetwood</a> was sent to commission the disafforestation and division of lands being used in common.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> Riots destroyed enclosures in spring 1627 and 1628, following a pattern of <a href="/wiki/Western_Rising_and_disafforestation_riots" title="Western Rising and disafforestation riots">anti-enclosure disturbances</a> found elsewhere including the Western Rising.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Petitions challenging the enclosures were presented by the Corporation of Leicester and borough residents to the King and <a href="/wiki/Privy_Council" class="mw-redirect" title="Privy Council">Privy Council</a>. They were unsuccessful so petitioned the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Lords" title="House of Lords">House of Lords</a> in June 1628 who however supported Fleetwood but asked for proceedings made by the Crown against the rioters to be dropped. Compensation made to the legal residents of the forest was reasonably generous by comparison with other forests. The Corporation received 40 acres (16&#160;ha) for relief of the poor.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Civil_War">Civil War</span></h4>\n<p>Leicester was a Parliamentarian (colloquially called <a href="/wiki/Roundhead" title="Roundhead">Roundhead</a>) stronghold during the <a href="/wiki/English_Civil_War" title="English Civil War">English Civil War</a>. In 1645, King <a href="/wiki/Charles_I_of_England" title="Charles I of England">Charles I of England</a> and <a href="/wiki/Prince_Rupert" class="mw-redirect" title="Prince Rupert">Prince Rupert</a> decided to attack the (then) town to draw the <a href="/wiki/New_Model_Army" title="New Model Army">New Model Army</a> away from the Royalist (colloquially called <a href="/wiki/Cavaliers" class="mw-redirect" title="Cavaliers">Cavaliers</a>) headquarters of <a href="/wiki/Oxford" title="Oxford">Oxford</a>. <a href="/wiki/Royalist" title="Royalist">Royalist</a> guns were set up on <a href="/wiki/Raw_Dykes" title="Raw Dykes">Raw Dykes</a> and, after an unsatisfactory response to a demand for surrender,the assault began at 3pm on 30 May 1645 by a Royalist battery opposite the Newarke. The city was sacked on 31 May 1645, and hundreds of people were killed by Rupert\'s cavalry. One witness said, \xe2\x80\x9cthey fired upon our men out of their windows, from the tops of houses, and threw tiles upon their heads. Finding one house better manned than ordinary, and many shots fired at us out of the windows, I caused my men to attack it, and resolved to make them an example for the rest; which they did. Breaking open the doors, they killed all they found there without distinction.\xe2\x80\x9d It was reported that 120 houses had been destroyed and that 140 wagons of plunder were sent to the Royalist stronghold of <a href="/wiki/Newark-on-Trent" title="Newark-on-Trent">Newark</a>.\n<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Following the Parliamentarian victory over the Royalists at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Naseby" title="Battle of Naseby">Battle of Naseby</a> on 14 June 1645 Leicester was recovered by Parliament on 18 June 1645.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Industrial_era">Industrial era</span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:112px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Butler_Leicester_Seamstress_front.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Butler_Leicester_Seamstress_front.jpg/110px-Butler_Leicester_Seamstress_front.jpg" width="110" height="196" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Butler_Leicester_Seamstress_front.jpg/165px-Butler_Leicester_Seamstress_front.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Butler_Leicester_Seamstress_front.jpg/220px-Butler_Leicester_Seamstress_front.jpg 2x" data-file-width="755" data-file-height="1343" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Butler_Leicester_Seamstress_front.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>The Leicester Seamstress</i> by <a href="/wiki/James_Butler_(artist)" title="James Butler (artist)">James Walter Butler</a> (1990)<br />Leicester, Hotel Street</div></div></div>\n<p>The construction of the <a href="/wiki/Grand_Union_Canal" title="Grand Union Canal">Grand Union Canal</a> in the 1790s linked Leicester to London and <a href="/wiki/Birmingham" title="Birmingham">Birmingham</a>. In 1832, the railway arrived in Leicester.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> in the form of The <a href="/wiki/Leicester_and_Swannington_Railway" title="Leicester and Swannington Railway">Leicester and Swannington Railway</a> which provided a supply of coal to the town from nearby collieries.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Midland_Counties_Railway" title="Midland Counties Railway">Midland Counties Railway</a>     (running from Derby to Rugby) linked the town to the national network by 1840. A direct link to London <a href="/wiki/St_Pancras_Station" class="mw-redirect" title="St Pancras Station">St Pancras Station</a> was established by the <a href="/wiki/Midland_Main_Line" title="Midland Main Line">Midland</a> Railway in the 1860s. These developments encouraged and accompanied a <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution_in_the_United_Kingdom" class="mw-redirect" title="Industrial Revolution in the United Kingdom">process of industrialisation</a> which intensified throughout the reign of <a href="/wiki/Queen_Victoria" title="Queen Victoria">Queen Victoria</a>. Factories began to appear, particularly along the canal and river, and districts such as <a href="/wiki/Frog_Island,_Leicester" title="Frog Island, Leicester">Frog Island</a> and <a href="/wiki/Woodgate,_Leicestershire" class="mw-redirect" title="Woodgate, Leicestershire">Woodgate</a> were the locations of numerous large mills. Between 1861 and 1901, Leicester\'s population increased from <span class="nowrap">68,100</span> to <span class="nowrap">211,600</span><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>and the proportion employed in trade, commerce, building, and the city\'s new factories and workshops rose steadily. Hosiery, textiles, and footwear became the major industrial employers: manufacturers such as <a href="/wiki/N._Corah_%26_Sons" title="N. Corah &amp; Sons">N. Corah &amp; Sons</a> and the Cooperative Boot and Shoe Company were opening some of the largest manufacturing premises in Europe. They were joined, in the latter part of the century, by engineering firms such as Kent Street\'s Taylor and Hubbard (crane makers and founders<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (December 2014)">clarification needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>), Vulcan Road\'s <a href="/wiki/Gimson_and_Company" title="Gimson and Company">William Gimson &amp; Company</a> (steam boilers and founders), and Martin Street\'s Richards &amp; Company (steel works and founders).\n</p><p>The politics of Victorian Leicester were lively and very often bitter. Years of consistent economic growth meant living standards generally increased, but Leicester was a stronghold of <a href="/wiki/Radicals_(UK)" title="Radicals (UK)">Radicalism</a>. <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Cooper_(poet)" title="Thomas Cooper (poet)">Thomas Cooper</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Chartism" title="Chartism">Chartist</a>, kept a shop in Church Gate.  There were serious Chartist riots in the town in 1842 and again six years later.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Leicester_Secular_Society" title="Leicester Secular Society">Leicester Secular Society</a> was founded in 1851 but <a href="/wiki/Secularism" title="Secularism">secularist</a> speakers such as <a href="/wiki/George_Holyoake" title="George Holyoake">George Holyoake</a> were often denied the use of speaking halls. It was not until 1881 that <a href="/wiki/Leicester_Secular_Hall" title="Leicester Secular Hall">Leicester Secular Hall</a> was opened. The second half of the 19th century also witnessed the creation of many other institutions, including the town council, the Royal Infirmary, and the Leicester Constabulary. It also benefited from general acceptance (and the Public Health Acts )<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> that municipal organisations had a responsibility to provide for the town\'s water supply, drainage, and sanitation. In 1853, backed with a guarrantee of dividends by the Corporation the Leicester Waterworks Company built a reservoir at Thornton for the supply of water to the town . This guarrantee was made possible by the Public Health Act 1847 and an amending local Act of Parliament of 1851. In 1866 another amending Act enabled the Corporation to take shares in the company to enable another reservoir at Cropston, completed in 1870. The Corporation was later able to buy the waterworks and build another reservoir at Swithland, completed in the 1890s.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Leicester became a <a href="/wiki/County_borough" title="County borough">county borough</a> in 1889, although it was abolished with the rest in 1974 as part of the <a href="/wiki/Local_Government_Act_1972" title="Local Government Act 1972">Local Government Act</a>. The city regained its unitary status apart from Leicestershire in 1997. The borough had been expanding throughout the 19th century, but grew most notably when it annexed <a href="/wiki/Belgrave,_Leicestershire" class="mw-redirect" title="Belgrave, Leicestershire">Belgrave</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aylestone" title="Aylestone">Aylestone</a>, <a href="/wiki/North_Evington" title="North Evington">North Evington</a>, <a href="/wiki/Knighton,_Leicestershire" class="mw-redirect" title="Knighton, Leicestershire">Knighton</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Stoneygate" title="Stoneygate">Stoneygate</a> in 1892.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_20th_century">Early 20th century</span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Clock_Tower_and_Eastgates_c1910.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Clock_Tower_and_Eastgates_c1910.jpg/220px-Clock_Tower_and_Eastgates_c1910.jpg" width="220" height="143" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Clock_Tower_and_Eastgates_c1910.jpg/330px-Clock_Tower_and_Eastgates_c1910.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Clock_Tower_and_Eastgates_c1910.jpg/440px-Clock_Tower_and_Eastgates_c1910.jpg 2x" data-file-width="6736" data-file-height="4384" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Clock_Tower_and_Eastgates_c1910.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Edwardian Leicester</div></div></div>\n<p>In 1900, the <a href="/wiki/Great_Central_Railway" title="Great Central Railway">Great Central Railway</a> provided another link to London, but the rapid population growth of the previous decades had already begun to slow by the time of Queen Victoria\'s death in 1901. <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a> and the subsequent epidemics had further impacts. Nonetheless, Leicester was finally recognised as a legal city once more in 1919 and, in 1927, again became a cathedral city on the consecration of <span class="nowrap">St Martin\'s</span> Church as the Cathedral. A second major extension to the boundaries following the changes in 1892 took place in 1935, with the annexation of the remainder of <a href="/wiki/Evington" title="Evington">Evington</a>, <a href="/wiki/Humberstone,_Leicestershire" class="mw-redirect" title="Humberstone, Leicestershire">Humberstone</a>, <a href="/wiki/Beaumont_Leys" title="Beaumont Leys">Beaumont Leys</a>, and part of <a href="/w/index.php?title=Braunstone_Park_and_Rowley_Fields&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Braunstone Park and Rowley Fields (page does not exist)">Braunstone</a>. A third major revision of the boundaries took place in 1966, with the net addition to the city of just over 450 acres (182&#160;ha). The boundary has remained unchanged since that time.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:War_Memorial_Leicester,_Summer_2009.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/War_Memorial_Leicester%2C_Summer_2009.jpg/220px-War_Memorial_Leicester%2C_Summer_2009.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/War_Memorial_Leicester%2C_Summer_2009.jpg/330px-War_Memorial_Leicester%2C_Summer_2009.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/War_Memorial_Leicester%2C_Summer_2009.jpg/440px-War_Memorial_Leicester%2C_Summer_2009.jpg 2x" data-file-width="7524" data-file-height="5016" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:War_Memorial_Leicester,_Summer_2009.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Arch Victorious, formerly \'The War Memorial\' in <a href="/wiki/Victoria_Park,_Leicester" title="Victoria Park, Leicester">Victoria Park</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Leicester\'s diversified economic base and lack of dependence on primary industries meant it was much better placed than many other cities to weather the tariff wars of the 1920s and <a href="/wiki/Great_Depression" title="Great Depression">Great Depression</a> of the 1930s. The Bureau of Statistics of the newly formed <a href="/wiki/League_of_Nations" title="League of Nations">League of Nations</a> identified Leicester in 1936 as the 2nd-richest city in Europe<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> and it became an attractive destination for refugees fleeing persecution and political turmoil in <a href="/wiki/Continental_Europe" title="Continental Europe">continental Europe</a>. Firms such as Corah and <a href="/w/index.php?title=Liberty_Shoes_(Leicester,_England)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Liberty Shoes (Leicester, England) (page does not exist)">Liberty Shoes</a> used their reputation for producing high-quality products to expand their businesses. These years witnessed the growth in the city of <a href="/wiki/Trade_unionism" class="mw-redirect" title="Trade unionism">trade unionism</a> and particularly the <a href="/wiki/Co-operative_movement" class="mw-redirect" title="Co-operative movement">co-operative movement</a>. The Co-op became an important employer and landowner; when Leicester played host to the <a href="/wiki/Jarrow_March" title="Jarrow March">Jarrow March</a> on its way to London in 1936, the Co-op provided the marchers with a change of boots. In 1938, Leicester was selected as the base for Squadron 1F, the first A.D.C.C (Air Defence Cadet Corp), the predecessor of the <a href="/wiki/Air_Training_Corps" title="Air Training Corps">Air Training Corps</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Contemporary">Contemporary</span></h3>\n<p>The years after <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, particularly from the 1960s onwards, brought many social and economic challenges.\n</p>\n<h4><span id="Urban_expansion.3B_central_rapprochement"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Urban_expansion;_central_rapprochement">Urban expansion; central rapprochement</span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:277px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Aerial-Leicester_2017.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Aerial-Leicester_2017.jpg/275px-Aerial-Leicester_2017.jpg" width="275" height="183" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Aerial-Leicester_2017.jpg/413px-Aerial-Leicester_2017.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Aerial-Leicester_2017.jpg/550px-Aerial-Leicester_2017.jpg 2x" data-file-width="810" data-file-height="539" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Aerial-Leicester_2017.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Central Leicester (looking WNW)</div></div></div>\n<p>Mass housebuilding continued across Leicester for some 30 years after 1945. Existing housing estates such as Braunstone were expanded, while several completely new estates \xe2\x80\x93 of both private and council tenure \xe2\x80\x93 were built.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> The last major development of this era was Beaumont Leys in the north of the city, which was developed in the 1970s as a mix of private and council housing.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> There was a steady decline in Leicester\'s traditional manufacturing industries and, in the city centre, working factories and light industrial premises have now been almost entirely replaced. Many former factories, including some on <a href="/wiki/Frog_Island,_Leicester" title="Frog Island, Leicester">Frog Island</a> and at <a href="/wiki/River_Soar#Donisthorpe_Mill" title="River Soar">Donisthorpe Mill</a>, have been badly damaged by fire. Rail and barge were finally eclipsed by automotive transport in the 1960s and 1970s: the Great Central and the Leicester and Swannington both closed and the northward extension of the <a href="/wiki/M1_motorway" title="M1 motorway">M1 motorway</a> linked Leicester into England\'s growing motorway network.  With the loss of much of the city\'s industry during the 1970s and 1980s, some of the old industrial jobs were replaced by new jobs in the service sector, particularly in retail. The opening of the Haymarket Shopping Centre in 1971 was followed by a number of new shopping centres in the city, including St Martin\'s Shopping Centre in 1984 and the Shire Shopping Centre in 1992.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> The Shires was subsequently expanded in September 2008 and rebranded as Highcross.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup>  By the 1990s, as well, Leicester\'s central position and good transport links had established it as a distribution centre; the southwestern area of the city has also attracted new service and manufacturing businesses.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Immigration">Immigration</span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Leicester_city_council_advert_on_Ugandan_Argus.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/ad/Leicester_city_council_advert_on_Ugandan_Argus.jpg/170px-Leicester_city_council_advert_on_Ugandan_Argus.jpg" width="170" height="212" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/a/ad/Leicester_city_council_advert_on_Ugandan_Argus.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="226" data-file-height="282" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Leicester_city_council_advert_on_Ugandan_Argus.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>1972 advertisement in the <i><a href="/wiki/New_Vision" class="mw-redirect" title="New Vision">Uganda Argus</a></i> newspaper to discourage Ugandan Asians from settling in Leicester</div></div></div>\n<p>Since World War II Leicester has experienced large scale immigration from across the world. Many Polish servicemen were prevented from returning to their homeland after the war by the communist regime<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>, and they established a small community in Leicester. Economic migrants from the <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Ireland" title="Republic of Ireland">Irish Republic</a> continued to arrive throughout the post war period. Immigrants from the <a href="/wiki/Indian_sub-continent" class="mw-redirect" title="Indian sub-continent">Indian sub-continent</a> began to arrive in the 1960s, their numbers boosted by <a href="/wiki/South_Asia" title="South Asia">Asians</a> arriving from Kenya and Uganda in the early 1970s.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1972, <a href="/wiki/Idi_Amin" title="Idi Amin">Idi Amin</a> announced that the entire <a href="/wiki/South_Asia" title="South Asia">Asian</a> community in Uganda had 90 days to leave the country.<sup id="cite_ref-uganda4_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-uganda4-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> Shortly thereafter, the Leicester City Council launched a campaign aimed at dissuading Ugandan Asians from migrating to the city.<sup id="cite_ref-uganda1_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-uganda1-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> The adverts did not have their intended effect, <a href="/wiki/Streisand_effect" title="Streisand effect">instead making more migrants aware</a> of the possibility of settling in Leicester.<sup id="cite_ref-Uganda2_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Uganda2-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> Nearly a quarter of initial Ugandan refugees (around 5000 to 6000) settled in Leicester, and by the end of the 1970s around another quarter of the initially dispersed refugees had made their way to Leicester.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> Officially, the adverts were taken out for fear that immigrants to Leicester would place pressure on city services and at least one person who was a city councillor at the time says he believes they were placed for racist reasons.<sup id="cite_ref-uganda3_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-uganda3-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> The initial advertisement was widely condemned, and taken as a marker of anti-Asian sentiment throughout Britain as a whole, although the attitudes that resulted in the initial advertisement were changed significantly in subsequent decades,<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> not least because the immigrants included the owners of many of "Uganda\'s most successful businesses."<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Forty years later, Leicester\'s mayor Sir <a href="/wiki/Peter_Soulsby" title="Peter Soulsby">Peter Soulsby</a> expressed his regret for the behaviour of the council at the time.<sup id="cite_ref-uganda3_50-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-uganda3-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the 1990s, a group of Dutch citizens of <a href="/wiki/Somali_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Somali people">Somali</a> origin settled in the city. Since the 2004 <a href="/wiki/Enlargement_of_the_European_Union" title="Enlargement of the European Union">enlargement of the European Union</a> a significant number of <a href="/wiki/East_European" class="mw-redirect" title="East European">East European</a> migrants have settled in the city. While some wards in the northeast of the city are more than 70% South Asian, wards in the west and south are all over 70% white. The <a href="/wiki/Commission_for_Racial_Equality" title="Commission for Racial Equality">Commission for Racial Equality</a> (CRE) had estimated that by 2011 Leicester would have approximately a 50% ethnic minority population, making it the first city in Britain not to have an indigenous <a href="/wiki/White_British" title="White British">white British</a> majority.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> This prediction was based on the growth of the ethnic minority populations between 1991 (Census 1991 28% ethnic minority) and 2001 (Census 2001 \xe2\x80\x93 36% ethnic minority). However Professor Ludi Simpson at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Manchester" title="University of Manchester">University of Manchester</a> School of Social Sciences said in September 2007 that the CRE had "made unsubstantiated claims and ignored government statistics" and that Leicester\'s immigrant and minority communities disperse to other places.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Leicester Multicultural Advisory Group<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> is a forum, set up in 2001 by the editor of the <i><a href="/wiki/Leicester_Mercury" title="Leicester Mercury">Leicester Mercury</a></i>, to co-ordinate community relations with members representing the council, police, schools, community and faith groups, and the media.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geography">Geography</span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_settlements_in_Leicestershire_by_population" title="List of settlements in Leicestershire by population">List of settlements in Leicestershire by population</a></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Leicester_Urban_Area" title="Leicester Urban Area">Leicester Urban Area</a></div>\n<p><br />\n</p>\n<table class="wikitable floatleft" style="border: 1px solid darkgray;">\n\n\n<tbody><tr>\n<td style="width: 800px;"><div class="mw-graph" style="min-width:800px;min-height:800px"><img class="mw-graph-img" src="/api/rest_v1/page/graph/png/Leicester/0/b028c46cd0d056c1e88fef4c5e5236eb583042eb.png" /></div>\n<div style="text-align: left; direction: ltr; font-size:70%; color:grey"><a class="mw-kartographer-maplink" mw-data="interface" data-style="osm-intl" href="/wiki/Special:Map/13/52.635/-1.122/en" data-zoom="13" data-lat="52.635" data-lon="-1.122" data-overlays="[&quot;_3a39d6b133c0eacc2f4fb6e0477bbfa3a458b849&quot;]">[Full screen]</a></div>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td style="width: 800px; font-size:90%">Map of Leicester showing some of the localities and suburbs\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Office_for_National_Statistics" title="Office for National Statistics">Office for National Statistics</a> has defined a <a href="/wiki/Leicester_Urban_Area" title="Leicester Urban Area">Leicester Urban Area</a> (LUA); broadly the immediate Leicester <a href="/wiki/Conurbation" title="Conurbation">conurbation</a>, although without administrative status. The LUA contains the unitary authority area and several towns, villages and suburbs outside the city\'s administrative boundaries.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Climate">Climate</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Spinney_Hills_Park,_Leicester.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Spinney_Hills_Park%2C_Leicester.jpg/170px-Spinney_Hills_Park%2C_Leicester.jpg" width="170" height="227" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Spinney_Hills_Park%2C_Leicester.jpg/255px-Spinney_Hills_Park%2C_Leicester.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Spinney_Hills_Park%2C_Leicester.jpg/340px-Spinney_Hills_Park%2C_Leicester.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2304" data-file-height="3072" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Spinney_Hills_Park,_Leicester.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Snow in Spinney Hill Park, 2007</div></div></div>\n<p>Leicester experiences a <a href="/wiki/Maritime_climate" class="mw-redirect" title="Maritime climate">maritime climate</a> with mild to warm summers and cool winters, rain spread throughout the year, and low sunshine levels. The nearest official Weather Station was Newtown Linford, about 5 miles (8.0&#160;km) northwest of Leicester city centre and just outside the edge of the urban area. However, observations stopped there in 2003.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> The current nearest weather station is Market Bosworth, about 10 miles (16&#160;km) west of the city centre.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p>The highest temperature recorded at Newtown Linford was 34.5&#160;\xc2\xb0C (94.1&#160;\xc2\xb0F) during August 1990,<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> although a temperature of 35.1&#160;\xc2\xb0C (95.2&#160;\xc2\xb0F) was achieved at Leicester University during August 2003.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> More typically the highest temperature would reach 28.7&#160;\xc2\xb0C (83.7&#160;\xc2\xb0F) \xe2\x80\x93 the average annual maximum.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> 11.3&#160;\xc2\xb0C (52.3&#160;\xc2\xb0F) days of the year should attain a temperature of 25.1&#160;\xc2\xb0C (77.2&#160;\xc2\xb0F) or above.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The lowest temperature recorded at Newtown Linford was \xe2\x88\x9216.1&#160;\xc2\xb0C (3.0&#160;\xc2\xb0F) during January 1963.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> Typically, 54.9&#160;air frosts will be recorded during the course of the year.\n</p><p>Rainfall averages 684.4&#160;mm per year,<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> with 1&#160;mm or more falling on 120.8&#160;days.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> All averages refer to the period 1971\xe2\x80\x932000.\n</p>\n<table class="wikitable collapsible" style="width:90%; color:#000000; border-left-width:medium">684.37<br /> (26.9437)\n</td></tr>\n\n\n\n\n<tr>\n<td colspan="14" style="text-align:center;font-size:95%;">Source: KNMI<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Government">Government</span></h2>\n<p>Leicester is divided into three Parliamentary constituencies, all controlled by <a href="/wiki/Labour_Party_(UK)" title="Labour Party (UK)">the Labour Party</a>&#160;: <a href="/wiki/Leicester_East_(UK_Parliament_constituency)" title="Leicester East (UK Parliament constituency)">Leicester East</a>, represented by <a href="/wiki/Keith_Vaz" title="Keith Vaz">Keith Vaz</a>, <a href="/wiki/Leicester_South_(UK_Parliament_constituency)" title="Leicester South (UK Parliament constituency)">Leicester South</a>, represented by <a href="/wiki/Jon_Ashworth" title="Jon Ashworth">Jon Ashworth</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Leicester_West_(UK_Parliament_constituency)" title="Leicester West (UK Parliament constituency)">Leicester West</a> represented by <a href="/wiki/Liz_Kendall" title="Liz Kendall">Liz Kendall</a>. In April 2011 the then Leicester South MP <a href="/wiki/Peter_Soulsby" title="Peter Soulsby">Sir Peter Soulsby</a> left the House of Commons to seek election as Mayor of Leicester.\n</p><p>On 5 May 2011, Peter Soulsby became the first <a href="/wiki/Directly_elected" class="mw-redirect" title="Directly elected">directly elected</a> <a href="/wiki/Mayor_of_Leicester" title="Mayor of Leicester">Mayor of Leicester</a>.\n</p><p>\nBefore the creation of an elected executive Mayor the post of civic mayor and later Lord Mayor existed. The first mayor of Leicester was a Norman knight, Peter fitz Roger ("Peter, son of Roger") in 1251.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> Following the restoration of city status this title was elevated to "Lord Mayor." In 1987 the first Asian Mayor of Leicester was indirectly elected by the councillors, Councillor Gordhan Parmar.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> After institution of a directly elected mayor in 2011 the Lord Mayor of Leicester still exists as a ceremonial role under <a href="/wiki/Leicester_City_Council" title="Leicester City Council">Leicester City Council</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup></p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Peter_Soulsby.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Peter_Soulsby.JPG/170px-Peter_Soulsby.JPG" width="170" height="254" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Peter_Soulsby.JPG/255px-Peter_Soulsby.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Peter_Soulsby.JPG/340px-Peter_Soulsby.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2592" data-file-height="3872" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Peter_Soulsby.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Sir_Peter_Soulsby" class="mw-redirect" title="Sir Peter Soulsby">Sir Peter Soulsby</a>, the first and current <a href="/wiki/Directly_elected_mayor_of_Leicester" class="mw-redirect" title="Directly elected mayor of Leicester">directly elected mayor of Leicester</a></div></div></div>\n<p>On 1 April 1997, <a href="/wiki/Leicester_City_Council" title="Leicester City Council">Leicester City Council</a> became a <a href="/wiki/Unitary_authority" title="Unitary authority">unitary authority</a>. Before then, local government was a two-tier system: the city and county councils were responsible for different aspects of local government services: this system is still in place in the rest of Leicestershire. <a href="/wiki/Leicestershire_County_Council" title="Leicestershire County Council">Leicestershire County Council</a> retained its headquarters at County Hall in <a href="/wiki/Glenfield,_Leicestershire" title="Glenfield, Leicestershire">Glenfield</a>, just outside the city boundary but within the urban area. The administrative offices of Leicester City Council are in the centre of the city at 115 Charles Street, having moved from Welford Place. The buildings at Welford Place have been demolished and the site is to be developed into a complex of offices and plazas. Some services (particularly the police and the ambulance service) still cover the whole of the city and county/ies, but for the most part the councils are independent.\n</p><p>Leicester is divided into 21 administrative wards: Abbey, Aylestone, Beaumont Leys, Belgrave, Braunstone Park &amp; Rowley Fields, Castle, Evington, Eyres Monsell, Fosse, Humberstone &amp; Hamilton, Knighton, North Evington, Rushey Mead, Saffron, Spinney Hills, Stoneygate, Thurncourt, Troon, Westcotes, Western, and Wycliffe.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>After a long period of Labour administration (since 1979), the city council from May 2003 was run by a <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Democrats_(UK)" title="Liberal Democrats (UK)">Liberal Democrat</a>/<a href="/wiki/Conservative_Party_(UK)" title="Conservative Party (UK)">Conservative</a> coalition under <a href="/wiki/Roger_Blackmore" title="Roger Blackmore">Roger Blackmore</a>, which collapsed in November 2004. The minority Labour group ran the city until May 2005, under Ross Willmott, when the Liberal Democrats and Conservatives formed a new coalition, again under the leadership of Roger Blackmore.\n</p><p>In the local government elections of 3 May 2007, Leicester\'s Labour Party once again took control of the council in what can be described as a <a href="/wiki/Landslide_victory" title="Landslide victory">landslide victory</a>. Gaining 18 new councillors, Labour polled on the day 38 councillors, creating a governing majority of +20. Significantly however, the Green Party gained its first councillors in the Castle Ward, after losing on the drawing of lots in 2003, though one of these subsequently resigned and the seat was lost to Labour in a by-election on 10 September 2009.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> The Conservative Party saw a decrease in their representation. The Liberal Democrat Party was the major loser, dropping from 25 councillors in 2003 to only 6 in 2007.\n</p><p>In the local government elections of 5 May 2011 and those of May 2015, Labour won 52 of the city\'s 54 seats, with the Conservatives and Liberal Democrats winning one seat each.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Political_control">Political control</span></h3>\n<p>The current composition of Leicester City Council is as follows:\n</p>\n<table class="wikitable" border="1">\n\n<tbody><tr>\n<th colspan="2" rowspan="1" align="center" valign="top"><b>Party</b>\n</th>\n<th valign="top"><b>Seats</b>\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td style="width:4px; background-color:#DC241f;" data-sort-value="Labour Party (UK)">\n</td>\n<td style="text-align:left;" scope="row"><a href="/wiki/Labour_Party_(UK)" title="Labour Party (UK)">Labour</a>\n</td>\n<td>51\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td style="width:4px; background-color:#0087DC;" data-sort-value="Conservative Party (UK)">\n</td>\n<td style="text-align:left;" scope="row"><a href="/wiki/Conservative_Party_(UK)" title="Conservative Party (UK)">Conservative</a>\n</td>\n<td>1\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td style="width:4px; background-color:#FAA61A;" data-sort-value="Liberal Democrats (UK)">\n</td>\n<td style="text-align:left;" scope="row"><a href="/wiki/Liberal_Democrats_(UK)" title="Liberal Democrats (UK)">Liberal Democrat</a>\n</td>\n<td>1\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td style="width:4px; background-color:#DDDDDD;" data-sort-value="Independent (politician)">\n</td>\n<td style="text-align:left;" scope="row"><a href="/wiki/Independent_(politician)" class="mw-redirect" title="Independent (politician)">Independent</a>\n</td>\n<td>1\n</td></tr>\n</tbody></table>\n<ul><li class="mw-empty-elt"></li></ul>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Coat_of_arms">Coat of arms</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Arms_of_Leicester.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Arms_of_Leicester.svg/220px-Arms_of_Leicester.svg.png" width="220" height="242" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Arms_of_Leicester.svg/330px-Arms_of_Leicester.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Arms_of_Leicester.svg/440px-Arms_of_Leicester.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="563" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Arms_of_Leicester.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Arms of the City of Leicester: <i>Gules, a cinquefoil ermine pierced of the field</i></div></div></div>\n<p>The Corporation of Leicester\'s <a href="/wiki/Coat_of_arms" title="Coat of arms">coat of arms</a> was first granted to the city at the Heraldic Visitation of 1619, and is based on the arms of the first <a href="/wiki/Earl_of_Leicester" title="Earl of Leicester">Earl of Leicester</a>, Robert Beaumont. The charge is a <a href="/wiki/Cinquefoil" class="mw-redirect" title="Cinquefoil">cinquefoil</a> ermine, on a red field, and this emblem is used by the city council.\n</p><p>After Leicester became a city again in 1919, the city council applied to add to the arms. Permission for this was granted in 1929, when the supporting lions, from the Lancastrian Earls of Leicester, were added.\n</p><p>The motto <i>"Semper Eadem"</i> was the motto of <a href="/wiki/Queen_Elizabeth_I" class="mw-redirect" title="Queen Elizabeth I">Queen Elizabeth I</a>, who granted a royal charter to the city. It means "always the same" but with positive overtones meaning unchanging, reliable or dependable, and united. The crest on top of the arms is a white or silver legless <a href="/wiki/Wyvern" title="Wyvern">wyvern</a> with red and white wounds showing, on a wreath of red and white. The legless wyvern distinguishes it as a Leicester wyvern as opposed to other wyverns. The supporting lions are wearing coronets in the form of collars, with the white cinquefoil hanging from them.\n</p>\n<div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Demography">Demography</span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Demography_of_Leicester" title="Demography of Leicester">Demography of Leicester</a></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Demographic_comparatives">Demographic comparatives</span></h3>\n<p>In the 2011 census, the population of the Leicester unitary authority was 329,839, an increase of 11.8% compared to the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_Census_2001" class="mw-redirect" title="United Kingdom Census 2001">United Kingdom Census 2001</a> figure of 279,921. The wider <a href="/wiki/Leicester_Urban_Area" title="Leicester Urban Area">Leicester Urban Area</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-BUA_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BUA-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> showed an estimated population of 509,000. The population of the Leicester unitary authority is marginally higher than that of Nottingham, while Nottingham has a higher urban area population compared to Leicester. <a href="/wiki/Eurostat" title="Eurostat">Eurostat</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/Larger_Urban_Zone" class="mw-redirect" title="Larger Urban Zone">Larger Urban Zone</a> listed the population of the Leicester LUZ at 836,484 (2011) just above that of their East Midland neighbour; metropolitan and city region populations tend to be similar. According to the 2011 census Leicester had the largest proportion of people aged 19-and-under in the East Midlands at 27 per cent. Coventry, to the south west, has a population of 352,900 (2016 est.) compared to Leicester\'s 348,300 at the same date. Nonetheless, Coventry has an area one third greater than Leicester\'s, approximately equivalent to a combined \'Leicester + <a href="/wiki/Oadby_and_Wigston" title="Oadby and Wigston">Oadby and Wigston</a>\' with a respective population of 404,100 (2016 est.).\n</p><p>The Eurostat regional yearbook 2015 classifies Leicester as one of country\'s eleven \'Greater Cities\', together with Birmingham and Nottingham in the Midlands. Leicester is second only to Bristol as the largest <a href="/wiki/Unitary_authority" title="Unitary authority">unitary authority</a> city in England (<a href="/wiki/List_of_English_districts_by_population" title="List of English districts by population">List of English districts by population</a> 2015 estimates), and ninth largest counting both unitary authority cities and cities within metropolitan counties.\n</p>\n<table class="wikitable" id="toc" style="float: right; margin-left: 2em; width: 40%; font-size: 85%;" cellspacing="3">\n<tbody><tr>\n<th colspan="4"><b>Leicester compared</b><sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup>\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><b><a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_Census_2011" class="mw-redirect" title="United Kingdom Census 2011">UK Census 2011</a></b></td>\n<td><b>Leicester</b></td>\n<td><b><a href="/wiki/East_Midlands" title="East Midlands">East Midlands</a></b></td>\n<td><b>England</b>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Total population</td>\n<td>329,839</td>\n<td>4,533,222</td>\n<td>53,012,456\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Foreign
